<th class="summary" colspan="2" style="background-color:#B0C4DE;text-align:center;vertical-align:middle;font-size:110%;">Xinhai Revolution (Revolution of 1911)</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="background-color:#DCDCDC;text-align:center;vertical-align:middle;">Part of <a href="/wiki/Anti-Qing_sentiment" title="Anti-Qing sentiment">Anti-Qing movement</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;border-bottom:1px solid #aaa;line-height:1.5em;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xinhai_Revolution_in_Shanghai.jpg"><img alt="Xinhai Revolution in Shanghai.jpg" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="639" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Xinhai_Revolution_in_Shanghai.jpg/300px-Xinhai_Revolution_in_Shanghai.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Xinhai_Revolution_in_Shanghai.jpg/450px-Xinhai_Revolution_in_Shanghai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Xinhai_Revolution_in_Shanghai.jpg/600px-Xinhai_Revolution_in_Shanghai.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a><br/>
Double Ten Revolution in Shanghai. The picture above is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanjing_Road_(Shanghai)" title="Nanjing Road (Shanghai)">Nanjing Road</a> after the <a href="#Shanghai_Armed_Uprising">Shanghai Uprising</a>, hung with the <a href="/wiki/Five_Races_Under_One_Union" title="Five Races Under One Union">Five Races Under One Union</a> flags then used by the revolutionaries.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2">
<table style="width:100%;margin:0;padding:0;border:0">
<tr>
<th style="padding-right:1em">Date</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/Wuchang_Uprising" title="Wuchang Uprising">10 October 1911<span style="display:none"> (<span class="bday dtstart published updated">1911-10-10</span>)</span></a> – 12 February 1912<span style="display:none"> (<span class="dtend">1912-02-12</span>)</span><br/>
(4 months and 2 days)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th style="padding-right:1em">Location</th>
<td><span class="location">China</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th style="padding-right:1em">Result</th>
<td>
<ul>
<li>Victory of the <a href="/wiki/Tongmenghui" title="Tongmenghui">Chinese Revolutionary Alliance</a></li>
<li>Abdication of <a href="/wiki/Puyi" title="Puyi">Puyi</a></li>
<li>Downfall of the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a></li>
<li>End of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_China" title="Imperial China">Imperial China</a></li>
<li>Establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Government_of_the_Republic_of_China_(1912)" title="Provisional Government of the Republic of China (1912)">Provisional Government of the Republic of China</a></li>
</ul>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>

<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Revolution_sidebar" title="Template:Revolution sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Revolution_sidebar" title="Template talk:Revolution sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Revolution_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>Xinhai Revolution</b> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">辛亥革命</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Xīnhài gémìng</span></i>), also known as the <b>Revolution of 1911</b>, or the <b>Chinese Revolution</b> (though this term may also refer to a number of <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Chinese_Revolution_(disambiguation)" title="Chinese Revolution (disambiguation)">other events</a>), was a revolution that overthrew China's last <a href="/wiki/Dynasties_in_Chinese_history#Dynasties" title="Dynasties in Chinese history">imperial dynasty</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a>), and established the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%9349)" title="Republic of China (1912–49)">Republic of China</a> (ROC). The revolution was named Xinhai (Hsin-hai) because it occurred in 1911, the year of the Xinhai (辛亥) stem-branch in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sexagenary_cycle" title="Sexagenary cycle">sexagenary cycle</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calendar" title="Chinese calendar">Chinese calendar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The revolution consisted of many revolts and uprisings. The turning point was the <a href="/wiki/Wuchang_Uprising" title="Wuchang Uprising">Wuchang Uprising</a> on October 10, 1911, that was a result of the mishandling of the <a href="/wiki/Railway_Protection_Movement" title="Railway Protection Movement">Railway Protection Movement</a>. The revolution ended with the abdication of the six-year-old "<a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">Last Emperor</a>", <a href="/wiki/Puyi" title="Puyi">Puyi</a>, on February 12, 1912, that marked the end of 2,000 years of <a href="/wiki/History_of_China#Imperial_era" title="History of China">imperial rule</a> and the beginning of China's <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Republic_of_China#Early_republic_.281912.E2.80.9316.29" title="History of the Republic of China">early republican era (1912–16)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xb1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-xb1-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>The revolution arose mainly in response to the decline of the Qing state, which had proven ineffective in its efforts to modernize China and confront foreign aggression. Many underground anti-Qing groups, with the support of Chinese revolutionaries in exile, tried to overthrow the Qing. The brief civil war that ensued was ended through a political compromise between <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Shikai" title="Yuan Shikai">Yuan Shikai</a>, the late Qing military strongman, and <a href="/wiki/Sun_Yat-sen" title="Sun Yat-sen">Sun Yat-sen</a>, the leader of the <a href="/wiki/Tongmenghui" title="Tongmenghui">Tongmenghui (United League)</a>. After the Qing court transferred power to the newly founded republic, a <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Government_of_the_Republic_of_China_(1912)" title="Provisional Government of the Republic of China (1912)">provisional coalition government</a> was created along with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_the_Republic_of_China" title="National Assembly of the Republic of China">National Assembly</a>. However, political power of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beiyang_Government" title="Beiyang Government">new national government</a> in Beijing was soon thereafter monopolized by Yuan and led to decades of political division and <a href="/wiki/Warlord_Era" title="Warlord Era">warlordism</a>, including several attempts at imperial restoration.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Republic_of_China" title="History of the Republic of China">Republic of China</a> in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland</a> consider themselves the legitimate successors to the Xinhai Revolution and revere the ideals of the revolution including <a href="/wiki/Chinese_nationalism" title="Chinese nationalism">nationalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republicanism</a>, modernization of China and <a href="/wiki/Zhonghua_minzu" title="Zhonghua minzu">national unity</a>. October 10 is commemorated in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_Ten_Day" title="Double Ten Day">Double Ten Day</a>, the National Day of the ROC. In mainland China, Hong Kong and <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the same day is usually celebrated as the <b>Anniversary of the Xinhai Revolution</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tvbs_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-tvbs-3">[3]</a></sup> Many <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Chinese" title="Overseas Chinese">overseas Chinese</a> also celebrate the anniversary in <a href="/wiki/Chinatown" title="Chinatown">Chinatowns</a> across the world.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:160px;max-width:160px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_in_the_1900s.PNG"><img alt="" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="567" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_in_the_1900s.PNG/158px-The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_in_the_1900s.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_in_the_1900s.PNG/237px-The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_in_the_1900s.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_in_the_1900s.PNG/316px-The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_in_the_1900s.PNG 2x" width="158"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:144px;max-width:144px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Postcard_of_Emperor_Guangxu.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="393" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Postcard_of_Emperor_Guangxu.jpg/142px-Postcard_of_Emperor_Guangxu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Postcard_of_Emperor_Guangxu.jpg/213px-Postcard_of_Emperor_Guangxu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Postcard_of_Emperor_Guangxu.jpg/284px-Postcard_of_Emperor_Guangxu.jpg 2x" width="142"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left/right/center;background-color:"><a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Cixi" title="Empress Dowager Cixi">Empress Dowager Cixi</a> (1835–1908), who personified the conservative Qing court and controlled court politics for 47 years, halted the attempt of her nephew, the <a href="/wiki/Guangxu_Emperor" title="Guangxu Emperor">Guangxu Emperor</a> (1871–1908), the penultimate Qing emperor, to institute reforms in 1898.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:350px;max-width:350px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:127px;max-width:127px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kang_Youwei_circa_1920.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="593" data-file-width="410" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Kang_Youwei_circa_1920.jpg/125px-Kang_Youwei_circa_1920.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Kang_Youwei_circa_1920.jpg/188px-Kang_Youwei_circa_1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Kang_Youwei_circa_1920.jpg/250px-Kang_Youwei_circa_1920.jpg 2x" width="125"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:124px;max-width:124px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MinistroDeJusticiaLiangQichao.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="921" data-file-width="630" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/MinistroDeJusticiaLiangQichao.jpg/122px-MinistroDeJusticiaLiangQichao.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/MinistroDeJusticiaLiangQichao.jpg/183px-MinistroDeJusticiaLiangQichao.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/MinistroDeJusticiaLiangQichao.jpg/244px-MinistroDeJusticiaLiangQichao.jpg 2x" width="122"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:93px;max-width:93px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tansitong.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="770" data-file-width="369" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Tansitong.jpg/91px-Tansitong.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Tansitong.jpg/137px-Tansitong.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Tansitong.jpg/182px-Tansitong.jpg 2x" width="91"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left/right/center;background-color:">After the failure of the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days%27_Reform" title="Hundred Days' Reform">Hundred Days' Reform</a> in 1898, Guangxu's advisors <a href="/wiki/Kang_Youwei" title="Kang Youwei">Kang Youwei</a> (left, 1858–1927) and <a href="/wiki/Liang_Qichao" title="Liang Qichao">Liang Qichao</a> (1873–1929) fled into exile, while <a href="/wiki/Tan_Sitong" title="Tan Sitong">Tan Sitong</a> (right, 1865–1898) was executed. In Canada, Kang and Liang formed the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Party_(China)" title="Progressive Party (China)">Emperor Protection Society</a> to promote constitutional monarchy for China. In 1900, they supported an unsuccessful uprising in central China to rescue Guangxu. After the Xinhai Revolution, Liang became a <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Minister_of_Justice_of_the_Republic_of_China&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Minister of Justice of the Republic of China (page does not exist)">Minister of Justice of the Republic of China</a>. Kang remained a royalist and supported <a href="/wiki/Puyi#Brief_restoration_.281917.29" title="Puyi">restoring</a> the last Qing emperor <a href="/wiki/Puyi" title="Puyi">Puyi</a> in 1917.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After suffering its first defeat to the West in the <a href="/wiki/First_Opium_War" title="First Opium War">First Opium War</a> in 1842, the Qing court struggled to contain foreign intrusions into China. Efforts to adjust and reform the traditional methods of governance were constrained by a deeply conservative court culture where ethnic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manchu" title="Manchu">Manchu</a> rulers did not want to give too much authority to the <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> majority.</p>
<p>In the wars against the <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">Taiping (1851–64)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nian_Rebellion" title="Nian Rebellion">Nian (1851–68)</a>, Muslims of <a href="/wiki/Panthay_Rebellion" title="Panthay Rebellion">Yunnan (1856–68)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dungan_revolt_(1862%E2%80%9377)" title="Dungan revolt (1862–77)">the Northwest (1862–77)</a>, the traditional <a href="/wiki/Eight_Banners" title="Eight Banners">Manchu armies</a> proved themselves incompetent, and the court came to rely on local Han armies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wangke106_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wangke106-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following defeat in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Opium_War" title="Second Opium War">Second Opium War</a>, the Qing tried to modernize by adopting certain Western technologies through the <a href="/wiki/Self-Strengthening_Movement" title="Self-Strengthening Movement">Self-Strengthening Movement</a> from 1861.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> In 1895, China suffered a serious defeat during the <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">First Sino-Japanese War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bevir_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bevir-6">[6]</a></sup> This demonstrated that traditional Chinese feudal society also needed to be modernized if the technological and commercial advancements were to succeed. In 1898 the <a href="/wiki/Guangxu_Emperor" title="Guangxu Emperor">Guangxu Emperor</a> was guided by reformers like <a href="/wiki/Kang_Youwei" title="Kang Youwei">Kang Youwei</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liang_Qichao" title="Liang Qichao">Liang Qichao</a> for a drastic reform in education, military and economy under the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days%27_Reform" title="Hundred Days' Reform">Hundred Days' Reform</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bevir_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bevir-6">[6]</a></sup> The reform was a failure, as it was ended prematurely by a conservative coup led by <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Cixi" title="Empress Dowager Cixi">Empress Dowager Cixi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The Guangxu Emperor, who had always been a puppet dependent on Cixi, was put under house arrest in June 1898.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wangke106_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wangke106-4">[4]</a></sup> Reformers Kang and Liang would be exiled. While in Canada, in June 1899, they tried to form the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Party_(China)" title="Progressive Party (China)">Emperor Protection Society</a> in an attempt to restore the emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wangke106_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wangke106-4">[4]</a></sup> Empress Dowager Cixi mainly controlled the Qing dynasty from this point on. The <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Rebellion" title="Boxer Rebellion">Boxer Rebellion</a> prompted another foreign invasion of Beijing in 1900 and the imposition of <a href="/wiki/Unequal_treaty" title="Unequal treaty">unequal treaty</a> terms, which carved away territories, created extraterritorial concessions and gave away trade privileges. Under internal and external pressure, the Qing court began to adopt some of the reforms. The Qing managed to maintain its monopoly on political power by suppressing, often with great brutality, all domestic rebellions. Dissidents could operate only in secret societies and underground organizations, in foreign concessions or in exile overseas.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization_for_revolution">Organization for revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Organization for revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leaders_of_Revive_China_Party.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="343" data-file-width="465" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3f/Leaders_of_Revive_China_Party.jpg/220px-Leaders_of_Revive_China_Party.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3f/Leaders_of_Revive_China_Party.jpg/330px-Leaders_of_Revive_China_Party.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3f/Leaders_of_Revive_China_Party.jpg/440px-Leaders_of_Revive_China_Party.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

From left to right: <a href="/wiki/Tse_Tsan-tai" title="Tse Tsan-tai">Tse Tsan-tai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yeung_Ku-wan" title="Yeung Ku-wan">Yeung Ku-wan</a> (President), Sun Yat-sen, three of the earliest revolutionaries</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earliest_groups">Earliest groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Earliest groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There were many revolutionaries and groups that wanted to overthrow the Qing government to re-establish a <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> government. The earliest revolutionary organizations were founded outside of China, such as <a href="/wiki/Yeung_Ku-wan" title="Yeung Ku-wan">Yeung Ku-wan</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Furen_Literary_Society" title="Furen Literary Society">Furen Literary Society</a>, created in Hong Kong in 1890. There were 15 members, including <a href="/wiki/Tse_Tsan-tai" title="Tse Tsan-tai">Tse Tsan-tai</a>, who did political satire such as "The Situation in the Far East", one of the first ever Chinese <a href="/wiki/Manhua" title="Manhua">manhua</a>, and who later became one of the core founders of the <i><a href="/wiki/South_China_Morning_Post" title="South China Morning Post">South China Morning Post</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sun Yat-sen's <a href="/wiki/Revive_China_Society" title="Revive China Society">Xingzhonghui (Revive China Society)</a> was established in <a href="/wiki/Honolulu" title="Honolulu">Honolulu</a> in 1894 with the main purpose of raising funds for revolutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The two organizations were merged in 1894.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Smaller_groups">Smaller groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Smaller groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Huaxinghui" title="Huaxinghui">Huaxinghui (China Revival Society)</a> was founded in 1904 with notables like <a href="/wiki/Huang_Xing" title="Huang Xing">Huang Xing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Shizhao" title="Zhang Shizhao">Zhang Shizhao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chen_Tianhua" title="Chen Tianhua">Chen Tianhua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Song_Jiaoren" title="Song Jiaoren">Song Jiaoren</a>, along with 100 others. Their motto was "Take one province by force, and inspire the other provinces to rise up".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Guangfuhui" title="Guangfuhui">Guangfuhui (Restoration Society)</a> was also founded in 1904, in Shanghai with <a href="/wiki/Cai_Yuanpei" title="Cai Yuanpei">Cai Yuanpei</a>. Other notable members include <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Binglin" title="Zhang Binglin">Zhang Binglin</a> and Tao Chengzhang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Despite professing the anti-Qing cause, the Guangfuhui was highly critical of Sun Yat-sen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wangke287_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wangke287-13">[13]</a></sup> One of the most famous female revolutionaries was <a href="/wiki/Qiu_Jin" title="Qiu Jin">Qiu Jin</a>, who fought for <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">women's rights</a> and was also from Guangfuhui.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wangke287_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wangke287-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>There were also many other minor revolutionary organizations, such as Lizhi Xuehui (勵志學會) in <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a>, Gongqianghui (公強會) in <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a>, Yiwenhui (益聞會) and Hanzudulihui (漢族獨立會) in <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a>, Yizhishe (易知社) in <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a>, Yuewanghui (岳王會) in <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a> and Qunzhihui (群智會/群智社) in Guangzhou.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>There were also criminal organizations that were anti-Manchu, including the <a href="/wiki/Green_Gang" title="Green Gang">Green Gang</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hongmen" title="Hongmen">Hongmen</a> Zhigongtang (致公堂).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Sun Yat-sen himself came in contact with the Hongmen, also known as <a href="/wiki/Tiandihui" title="Tiandihui">Tiandihui (Heaven and Earth society)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gelaohui" title="Gelaohui">Gelaohui (Elder Brother society)</a> was another group, with <a href="/wiki/Zhu_De" title="Zhu De">Zhu De</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wu_Yuzhang" title="Wu Yuzhang">Wu Yuzhang</a>, Liu Zhidan (劉志丹) and <a href="/wiki/He_Long" title="He Long">He Long</a>. This is the revolutionary group that would eventually develop a strong link with the later <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China" title="Communist Party of China">Communist Party</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sun_Yat_Sen_together_with_the_members_of_the_Singapore_Branch_of_Tongmen_Hui.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1458" data-file-width="2255" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Sun_Yat_Sen_together_with_the_members_of_the_Singapore_Branch_of_Tongmen_Hui.png/250px-Sun_Yat_Sen_together_with_the_members_of_the_Singapore_Branch_of_Tongmen_Hui.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Sun_Yat_Sen_together_with_the_members_of_the_Singapore_Branch_of_Tongmen_Hui.png/375px-Sun_Yat_Sen_together_with_the_members_of_the_Singapore_Branch_of_Tongmen_Hui.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Sun_Yat_Sen_together_with_the_members_of_the_Singapore_Branch_of_Tongmen_Hui.png/500px-Sun_Yat_Sen_together_with_the_members_of_the_Singapore_Branch_of_Tongmen_Hui.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sun_Yat_Sen_together_with_the_members_of_the_Singapore_Branch_of_Tongmen_Hui.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sun Yat-sen with his <a href="/wiki/Tongmenghui" title="Tongmenghui">Tongmenghui</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tongmenghui">Tongmenghui</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Tongmenghui">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sun Yat-sen successfully united the Revive China Society, Huaxinghui and Guangfuhui in the summer of 1905, thereby establishing the unified <a href="/wiki/Tongmenghui" title="Tongmenghui">Tongmenghui (United League)</a> in August 1905 in Tokyo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chinahistvol1_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-chinahistvol1-18">[18]</a></sup> While it started in Tokyo, it had loose organizations distributed across and outside the country. Sun Yat-sen was the leader of this unified group. Other revolutionaries who worked with the Tongmenghui include <a href="/wiki/Wang_Jingwei" title="Wang Jingwei">Wang Jingwei</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hu_Hanmin" title="Hu Hanmin">Hu Hanmin</a>. When the Tongmenhui was established, more than 90% of the Tongmenhui members were between 17-26 years of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Some of the work in the era includes manhua publications, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_Current_Pictorial" title="Journal of Current Pictorial">Journal of Current Pictorial</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_groups">Later groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Later groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In February 1906 Rizhihui (日知會) also had many revolutionaries, including Sun Wu (孫武), Zhang Nanxian (張難先), He Jiwei and Feng Mumin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> A nucleus of attendees of this conference evolved into the Tongmenhui's establishment in Hubei.</p>
<p>In July 1907 several members of Tongmenhui in Tokyo advocated a revolution in the area of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze River</a>. Liu Quiyi (劉揆一), Jiao Dafeng (焦達峰), Zhang Boxiang (張伯祥) and Sun Wu (孫武) established Gongjinhui (Progressive Association) (共進會).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang390_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang390-24">[24]</a></sup> In January 1911, the revolutionary group Zhengwu Xueshe (振武學社) was renamed as Wenxueshe (Literary society) (文學社).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mz_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-mz-25">[25]</a></sup> Jiang Yiwu (蔣翊武) was chosen as the leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> These two organizations would play a big role in the Wuchang Uprising.</p>
<p>Many young revolutionaries adopted the <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_in_China" title="Anarchism in China">radical programs of the anarchists</a>. In Tokyo <a href="/wiki/Liu_Shipei" title="Liu Shipei">Liu Shipei</a> proposed the overthrow of the Manchus and a return to Chinese classical values. In Paris Li Shizhen, <a href="/wiki/Wu_Zhihui" title="Wu Zhihui">Wu Zhihui</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Renjie" title="Zhang Renjie">Zhang Renjie</a> agreed with Sun on the necessity of revolution and joined the Tongmenghui, but argued that a political replacement of one government with another government would not be progress; revolution in family, gender and social values would remove the need for government and coercion. <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Ji_(Republic_of_China)" title="Zhang Ji (Republic of China)">Zhang Ji</a> was among the anarchists who defended assassination and terrorism as a means toward revolution, but others insisted that only education was justifiable. Important anarchists included Cai Yuanpei, Wang Jingwei and Zhang Renjie, who gave Sun major financial help. Many of these anarchists would later assume high positions in the <a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">Kuomintang</a> (KMT).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Views">Views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anti-Qing_sentiment" title="Anti-Qing sentiment">Anti-Qing sentiment</a></div>
<p>Many revolutionaries promoted anti-Qing/anti-Manchu sentiments and revived memories of conflict between the ethnic minority <a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">Manchu</a> and the ethnic majority <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> from the late <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> (1368–1644). Leading intellectuals were influenced by books that had survived from the last years of the Ming dynasty, the last dynasty of Han Chinese. In 1904, Sun Yat-sen announced that his organization's goal was "to expel the <a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">Tatar barbarians</a>, to revive <a href="/wiki/Names_of_China#Zhongguo_and_Zhonghua" title="Names of China">Zhonghua</a>, to establish a Republic, and to distribute land equally among the people." (驅除韃虜, 恢復中華, 創立民國, 平均地權).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chinahistvol1_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-chinahistvol1-18">[18]</a></sup> Many of the underground groups promoted the ideas of "Resist Qing and restore Ming" (反清復明) that had been around since the days of the <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">Taiping Rebellion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Others, such as <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Binglin" title="Zhang Binglin">Zhang Binglin</a>, supported straight-up lines like "slay the manchus" and concepts like "Anti-Manchuism" (興漢滅胡 / 排滿主義).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Strata_and_groups">Strata and groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Strata and groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Xinhai Revolution was supported by many groups, including students and intellectuals who returned from abroad, as well as participants of the revolutionary organizations, overseas Chinese, soldiers of the new army, local gentry, farmers and others.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overseas_Chinese">Overseas Chinese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Overseas Chinese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_revolutionary_activities_in_Malaya" title="Chinese revolutionary activities in Malaya">Chinese revolutionary activities in Malaya</a></div>
<p>Assistance from <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Chinese" title="Overseas Chinese">overseas Chinese</a> was important in the Xinhai Revolution. In 1894, the first year of the Revive China Society, the first meeting ever held by the group was held in the home of Ho Fon, an overseas Chinese who was the leader of the first Chinese Church of Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Overseas Chinese supported and actively participated in the funding of revolutionary activities, especially the Southeast Asia Chinese of <a href="/wiki/British_Malaya" title="British Malaya">Malaya</a> (<a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gao_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gao-31">[31]</a></sup> Many of these groups were reorganized by Sun, who was referred to as the "father of the Chinese revolution".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gao_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gao-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Newly_emerged_intellectuals">Newly emerged intellectuals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Newly emerged intellectuals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1906, after the abolition of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_examinations" title="Imperial examinations">imperial examinations</a>, the Qing government established many new schools and encouraged students to study abroad. Many young people attended the new schools or went abroad to study in places like Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenby_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-fenby-32">[32]</a></sup> A new class of intellectuals emerged from those students, who contributed immensely to the Xinhai Revolution. Besides Sun Yat-sen, key figures in the revolution, such as Huang Xing, <a href="/wiki/Song_Jiaoren" title="Song Jiaoren">Song Jiaoren</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hu_Hanmin" title="Hu Hanmin">Hu Hanmin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liao_Zhongkai" title="Liao Zhongkai">Liao Zhongkai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Zhixin" title="Zhu Zhixin">Zhu Zhixin</a> and Wang Jingwei, were all Chinese students in Japan. Some were young students like <a href="/wiki/Zou_Rong" title="Zou Rong">Zou Rong</a>, known for writing the book <i>Revolutionary Army</i>, in which he talked about the extermination of the Manchus for the 260 years of oppression, sorrow, cruelty and tyranny and turning the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descendants_of_Yan_%26_Huang_Emperors" title="Descendants of Yan &amp; Huang Emperors">sons and grandsons of Yellow Emperor</a> into <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washingtons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenby109_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-fenby109-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Before 1908, revolutionaries focused on coordinating these organizations in preparation for uprisings that these organizations would launch; hence, these groups would provide most of the manpower needed for the overthrow of the Qing Dynasty. After the Xinhai Revolution, Sun Yat-sen recalled the days of recruiting support for the revolution and said, "The literati were deeply into the search for honors and profits, so they were regarded as having only secondary importance. By contrast, organizations like <a href="/wiki/Tiandihui" title="Tiandihui">Sanhehui</a> were able to sow widely the ideas of resisting the Qing and restoring the Ming."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gentry_and_businessmen">Gentry and businessmen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Gentry and businessmen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E7%9A%87%E6%97%8F%E5%85%A7%E9%96%A3.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="831" data-file-width="1183" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/%E7%9A%87%E6%97%8F%E5%85%A7%E9%96%A3.png/220px-%E7%9A%87%E6%97%8F%E5%85%A7%E9%96%A3.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/%E7%9A%87%E6%97%8F%E5%85%A7%E9%96%A3.png/330px-%E7%9A%87%E6%97%8F%E5%85%A7%E9%96%A3.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/%E7%9A%87%E6%97%8F%E5%85%A7%E9%96%A3.png/440px-%E7%9A%87%E6%97%8F%E5%85%A7%E9%96%A3.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E7%9A%87%E6%97%8F%E5%85%A7%E9%96%A3.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Yikuang,_Prince_Qing" title="Yikuang, Prince Qing">Prince Qing</a> with some <a href="/wiki/Prince_Qing%27s_Cabinet" title="Prince Qing's Cabinet">royal cabinet</a> members</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The strength of the gentry in local politics had become apparent. From December 1908, the Qing government created some apparatus to allow the gentry and businessmen to participate in politics. These middle-class people were originally supporters of constitutionalism. However, they became disenchanted when the Qing government created a <a href="/wiki/Prince_Qing%27s_Cabinet" title="Prince Qing's Cabinet">cabinet</a> with <a href="/wiki/Yikuang,_Prince_Qing" title="Yikuang, Prince Qing">Prince Qing</a> as <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_Imperial_Cabinet" title="Prime Minister of the Imperial Cabinet">prime minister</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> By early 1911, an experimental cabinet had thirteen members, nine of whom were Manchus selected from the imperial family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wangke76_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wangke76-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreigners">Foreigners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Foreigners">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Besides Chinese and overseas Chinese, some of the supporters and participants of the Xinhai Revolution were foreigners; among them, the Japanese were the most active group. Some Japanese even became members of Tongmenghui. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miyazaki_Touten" title="Miyazaki Touten">Miyazaki Touten</a> was the closest Japanese supporter; others included Heiyama Shu and <a href="/wiki/Ry%C5%8Dhei_Uchida" title="Ryōhei Uchida">Ryōhei Uchida</a>. <a href="/wiki/Homer_Lea" title="Homer Lea">Homer Lea</a>, an American, who became Sun Yat-sen's closest foreign advisor in 1910, supported Sun Yat-sen's military ambitions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> British soldier Rowland J. Mulkern also took part in the revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laukit_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laukit-38">[38]</a></sup> Some foreigners, such as English explorer <a href="/wiki/Arthur_de_Carle_Sowerby" title="Arthur de Carle Sowerby">Arthur de Carle Sowerby</a>, led expeditions to rescue foreign missionaries in 1911 and 1912.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borst-Smith_1912_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borst-Smith_1912-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">

</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:232px;max-width:232px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beiyang_Army.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="334" data-file-width="480" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Beiyang_Army.jpg/230px-Beiyang_Army.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Beiyang_Army.jpg/345px-Beiyang_Army.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Beiyang_Army.jpg/460px-Beiyang_Army.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left/right/center;background-color:">Yuan rose to power in north China and built the <a href="/wiki/Beiyang_Army" title="Beiyang Army">Beiyang Army</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soldiers_of_the_new_armies">Soldiers of the new armies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Soldiers of the new armies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/New_Army" title="New Army">New Army</a> was formed in 1901 after the defeat of the Qings in the <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">First Sino-Japanese War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenby_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-fenby-32">[32]</a></sup> They were launched by a decree from eight provinces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenby_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-fenby-32">[32]</a></sup> New Army troops were by far the best trained and equipped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenby_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-fenby-32">[32]</a></sup> The recruits were of a higher quality than the old army and received regular promotions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenby_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-fenby-32">[32]</a></sup> Beginning in 1908, the revolutionaries began to shift their call to the new armies. Sun Yat-sen and the revolutionaries infiltrated the New Army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uprisings_and_incidents">Uprisings and incidents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Uprisings and incidents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The central focus of the uprisings were mostly connected with the <a href="/wiki/Tongmenghui" title="Tongmenghui">Tongmenghui</a> and Sun Yat-sen, including subgroups. Some uprisings involved groups that never merged with the Tongmenghui. Sun Yat-sen may have participated in 8–10 uprisings; all uprisings prior to the failed Wuchang Uprising.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Naval_Jack_of_the_Republic_of_China.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Naval_Jack_of_the_Republic_of_China.svg/200px-Naval_Jack_of_the_Republic_of_China.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Naval_Jack_of_the_Republic_of_China.svg/300px-Naval_Jack_of_the_Republic_of_China.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Naval_Jack_of_the_Republic_of_China.svg/400px-Naval_Jack_of_the_Republic_of_China.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Naval_Jack_of_the_Republic_of_China.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Flag of the First Guangzhou Uprising</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Guangzhou_Uprising">First Guangzhou Uprising</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: First Guangzhou Uprising">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the spring of 1895, the <a href="/wiki/Revive_China_Society" title="Revive China Society">Revive China Society</a>, which was based in Hong Kong, planned the First Guangzhou Uprising (廣州起義). <a href="/wiki/Lu_Haodong" title="Lu Haodong">Lu Haodong</a> was tasked with designing the revolutionaries' <a href="/wiki/Blue_Sky_with_a_White_Sun" title="Blue Sky with a White Sun">Blue Sky with a White Sun</a> flag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gao_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gao-31">[31]</a></sup> On October 26, 1895, <a href="/wiki/Yeung_Ku-wan" title="Yeung Ku-wan">Yeung Ku-wan</a> and Sun Yat-sen led <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_Shiliang&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Zheng Shiliang (page does not exist)">Zheng Shiliang</a> and Lu Haodong to Guangzhou, preparing to capture Guangzhou in one strike. However, the details of their plans were leaked to the Qing government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-z1_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-z1-41">[41]</a></sup> The government began to arrest revolutionaries, including Lu Haodong, who was later executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-z1_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-z1-41">[41]</a></sup> The first Guangzhou uprising was a failure. Under pressure from the Qing government, the government of Hong Kong forbade these two men to enter the territory for five years. Sun Yat-sen went into exile, promoting the Chinese revolution and raising funds in Japan, the United States, Canada and Britain. In 1901, following the Huizhou uprising, Yeung Ku-wan was assassinated by Qing agents in Hong Kong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scmpYkw_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-scmpYkw-42">[42]</a></sup> After his death, his family protected his identity by not putting his name on his tomb, just a number: 6348.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scmpYkw_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-scmpYkw-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_Army_Uprising">Independence Army Uprising</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Independence Army Uprising">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1901, after the <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Rebellion" title="Boxer Rebellion">Boxer Rebellion</a> started, Tang Caichang (唐才常) and <a href="/wiki/Tan_Sitong" title="Tan Sitong">Tan Sitong</a> of the previous <a href="/wiki/Foot_Emancipation_Society" title="Foot Emancipation Society">Foot Emancipation Society</a> organised the Independence Army. The Independence Army Uprising (自立軍起義) was planned to occur on August 23, 1900.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang424_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang424-43">[43]</a></sup> Their goal was to overthrow <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Cixi" title="Empress Dowager Cixi">Empress Dowager Cixi</a> to establish a constitutional monarchy under the Guangxu Emperor. Their plot was discovered by the governor general of Hunan and Hubei. About twenty conspirators were arrested and executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang424_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wang424-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Huizhou_Uprising">Huizhou Uprising</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Huizhou Uprising">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On October 8, 1900, Sun Yat-sen ordered the launch of the <a href="/wiki/Huizhou" title="Huizhou">Huizhou</a> Uprising (惠州起義).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The revolutionary army was led by Zheng Shiliang and initially included 20,000 men, who fought for half a month. However, after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hirobumi_Ito" title="Hirobumi Ito">Japanese Prime Minister</a> prohibited Sun Yat-sen from carrying out revolutionary activities on Taiwan, Zheng Shiliang had no choice but to order the army to disperse. This uprising therefore also failed. British soldier Rowland J. Mulkern participated in this uprising.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laukit_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Laukit-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:236px;max-width:236px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:112px;max-width:112px">
