<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abdication_of_the_emperor">Abdication of the emperor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: Abdication of the emperor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Puyi#Abdication" title="Puyi">Puyi § Abdication</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Zhang_Jian_(politician)" title="Zhang Jian (politician)">Zhang Jian</a> drafted an abdication proposal that was approved by the Provisional Senate. On January 20, <a href="/wiki/Wu_Tingfang" title="Wu Tingfang">Wu Tingfang</a> of the Nanking Provisional government officially delivered the imperial edict of abdication to Yuan Shikai for the <a href="/wiki/Abdication" title="Abdication">abdication</a> of Puyi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rhoads228_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rhoads228-115">[115]</a></sup> On January 22, Sun Yat-sen announced that he would resign the presidency in favor of Yuan Shikai if the latter supported the emperor's abdication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Yuan then pressured <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Longyu" title="Empress Dowager Longyu">Empress Dowager Longyu</a> with the threat that the lives of the imperial family would not be spared if abdication did not come before the revolutionaries reached Beijing, but if they agree to abdicate, the provisional government would honor the terms proposed by the imperial family.</p>
<p>On February 3, Empress Dowager Longyu gave Yuan full permission to negotiate the abdication terms of the Qing emperor. Yuan then drew up his own version and forwarded it to the revolutionaries on February 3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rhoads228_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rhoads228-115">[115]</a></sup> His version consisted of three sections instead of two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rhoads228_115-3"><a href="#cite_note-Rhoads228-115">[115]</a></sup> On February 12, 1912, after being pressured by Yuan and other ministers, Puyi (age six) and Empress Dowager Longyu accepted Yuan's terms of abdication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Well_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Well-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Debate_over_the_capital">Debate over the capital</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: Debate over the capital">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Beijing" title="History of Beijing">History of Beijing</a></div>
<p>As a condition for ceding leadership to Yuan Shikai, Sun Yat-sen insisted that the provisional government remain in Nanjing. On February 14, the Provisional Senate initially voted 20-5 in favor of making Beijing the capital over Nanjing, with two votes going for Wuhan and one for Tianjin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xinhai.org_p.1_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xinhai.org_p.1-131">[131]</a></sup> The Senate majority wanted to secure the peace agreement by taking power in Beijing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xinhai.org_p.1_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-Xinhai.org_p.1-131">[131]</a></sup> Zhang Jian and others reasoned that having the capital in Beijing would check against Manchu restoration and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Outer_Mongolia_(1911%E2%80%9319)" title="Outer Mongolia (1911–19)">Mongol secession</a>. But Sun and <a href="/wiki/Huang_Xing" title="Huang Xing">Huang Xing</a> argued in favor of Nanjing to balance against Yuan's power base in the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xinhai.org_p.1_131-2"><a href="#cite_note-Xinhai.org_p.1-131">[131]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Li_Yuanhong" title="Li Yuanhong">Li Yuanhong</a> presented Wuhan as a compromise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> The next day, the Provisional Senate voted again, this time, 19-6 in favor of Nanjing with two votes for Wuhan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xinhai.org_p.1_131-3"><a href="#cite_note-Xinhai.org_p.1-131">[131]</a></sup> Sun sent a delegation led by Cai Yuanpei and Wang Jingwei to persuade Yuan to move to Nanjing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xinhai.org_p.3_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xinhai.org_p.3-133">[133]</a></sup> Yuan welcomed the delegation and agreed to accompany the delegates back to the south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xinhai.org_p.4_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xinhai.org_p.4-134">[134]</a></sup> Then on the evening of February 29, <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Government_of_the_Republic_of_China_(1912)#Northern_transition.23Dong.27anmen_Gate_incident" title="Provisional Government of the Republic of China (1912)">riots and fires</a> broke out all over the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xinhai.org_p.4_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Xinhai.org_p.4-134">[134]</a></sup> They were allegedly started by disobedient troops of <a href="/wiki/Cao_Kun" title="Cao Kun">Cao Kun</a>, a loyal officer of Yuan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xinhai.org_p.4_134-2"><a href="#cite_note-Xinhai.org_p.4-134">[134]</a></sup> The disorder gave Yuan the pretext to stay in the north to guard against unrest. On March 10, Yuan was inaugurated in Beijing as the provisional president of the Republic of China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xinhai.org_p.2_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xinhai.org_p.2-135">[135]</a></sup> On April 5, the Provisional Senate in Nanjing voted to make Beijing the capital of the Republic and convened in Beijing at the end of the month.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Republican_government_in_Beijing">Republican government in Beijing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: Republican government in Beijing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beiyang_Government" title="Beiyang Government">Beiyang Government</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yuan_Shikai_sworn_in_as_Provisional_President_-_10_March_1912.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="424" data-file-width="750" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Yuan_Shikai_sworn_in_as_Provisional_President_-_10_March_1912.jpg/220px-Yuan_Shikai_sworn_in_as_Provisional_President_-_10_March_1912.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Yuan_Shikai_sworn_in_as_Provisional_President_-_10_March_1912.jpg/330px-Yuan_Shikai_sworn_in_as_Provisional_President_-_10_March_1912.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Yuan_Shikai_sworn_in_as_Provisional_President_-_10_March_1912.jpg/440px-Yuan_Shikai_sworn_in_as_Provisional_President_-_10_March_1912.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yuan_Shikai_sworn_in_as_Provisional_President_-_10_March_1912.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Yuan Shikai sworn as the Provisional president in <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On March 10, 1912, Yuan Shikai was sworn as the second Provisional President of the Republic of China in Beijing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> The government based in Beijing, called the Beiyang Government, was not internationally recognized as the legitimate government of the Republic of China until 1928, so the period from 1912 until 1928 was known simply as the "Beiyang Period". The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_National_Assembly_elections,_1912" title="Republic of China National Assembly elections, 1912">first National Assembly election</a> took place according to the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Constitution_of_the_Republic_of_China" title="Provisional Constitution of the Republic of China">Provisional Constitution</a>. While in Beijing, the Kuomintang was formed on August 25, 1912.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> The KMT held the majority of seats after the election. <a href="/wiki/Song_Jiaoren" title="Song Jiaoren">Song Jiaoren</a> was elected as premier. However, Song was assassinated in Shanghai on March 20, 1913, under the secret order of Yuan Shikai.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_Han_monarchs_and_retention_of_aristocratic_noble_titles">Proposed Han monarchs and retention of aristocratic noble titles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: Proposed Han monarchs and retention of aristocratic noble titles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some advocated that a Han be installed as Emperor, either the descendant of Confucius, who was the <a href="/wiki/Duke_Yansheng" title="Duke Yansheng">Duke Yansheng</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woodhouse2004_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhouse2004-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spence1982_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spence1982-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HuLiu1983_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-HuLiu1983-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> or the Ming dynasty Imperial family descendant, the <a href="/wiki/Marquis_of_Extended_Grace" title="Marquis of Extended Grace">Marquis of Extended Grace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kent1912_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kent1912-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aldrich2008_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aldrich2008-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Han hereditary aristocratic nobility like the Duke Yansheng, Marquis of Extended Grace, and the title of the Wujing Boshi (changed to "Dacheng Zhisheng Xianshi Nanzong Fengsi Guan" 大成至聖先師南宗奉祀官) and the titles held by the descendants of <a href="/wiki/Mencius" title="Mencius">Mencius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zengzi" title="Zengzi">Zengzi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yan_Hui" title="Yan Hui">Yan Hui</a> were retained by the new Republic of China and the title holders continued to receive their pensions.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_views">Western views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: Western views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The American Christian Rev. Dr. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_F._Pentecost" title="George F. Pentecost">George F. Pentecost</a> spoke out against western imperialism, saying: <i>As for the Chinese, I have the highest opinion not only of the Chinese character, but of the Chinese fitness for self-government. I think they are eminently fitted to make a republic successful. China, for instance, is infinitely better fitted than is Russia for development along republican lines. In fact, China has always been practically a republic. It has had its dynasties of rulers, but the political unit of China has always been the village. The village people have always had their influence upon the Government. What is more, the average Chinaman is intelligent.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=67" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_influence">Social influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=68" title="Edit section: Social influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the revolution, there was a huge outpouring of anti-Manchu sentiment through China, but particularly in Beijing where thousands died in anti-Manchu violence as Imperial restrictions on Han residency and behavior within the city crumbled as Manchu Imperial power crumbled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Anti-Manchu sentiment is recorded in books like <i>A Short History of <a href="/wiki/Nucai" title="Nucai">Slaves</a></i> (奴才小史) and <i>The Biographies of Avaricious Officials and Corrupt Personnel</i> (貪官污吏傳) by Laoli (老吏).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rhoads266_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rhoads266-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ckd_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-ckd-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the abdication of the last emperor, Empress Dowager Longyu, Yuan Shikai and Sun Yat-sen both tried to adopt the concept of "Manchu and Han as one family" (滿漢一家).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rhoads266_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rhoads266-148">[148]</a></sup> People started exploring and debating with themselves on the root cause of their national weakness. This new search of identity was the <a href="/wiki/New_Culture_Movement" title="New Culture Movement">New Culture Movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Manchu culture and <a href="/wiki/Manchu_language" title="Manchu language">language</a>, on the contrary, has become virtually extinct by 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unlike revolutions in the West, the Xinhai Revolution did not restructure society. The participants of the Xinhai Revolution were mostly military personnel, old-type bureaucrats, and local gentries. These people still held regional power after the Xinhai Revolution. Some became warlords. There were no major improvements in the <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">standard of living</a>. Writer <a href="/wiki/Lu_Xun" title="Lu Xun">Lu Xun</a> commented in 1921 during the publishing of <i><a href="/wiki/The_True_Story_of_Ah_Q" title="The True Story of Ah Q">The True Story of Ah Q</a></i>, ten years after the Xinhai Revolution, that basically nothing changed except "the Manchus have left the kitchen".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> The economic problems were not addressed until the governance of <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Ching-kuo" title="Chiang Ching-kuo">Chiang Ching-kuo</a> in Taiwan and <a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a> on the mainland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scmpjul4_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-scmpjul4-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Xinhai Revolution mainly got rid of <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a> (<a href="/wiki/Fengjian" title="Fengjian">fengjian</a>) from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Imperial_China" title="Late Imperial China">Late Imperial China</a>. In the usual view of historians, there are two restorations of feudal power after the revolution: the first was Yuan Shikai; the second was <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Xun_(Qing_loyalist)" title="Zhang Xun (Qing loyalist)">Zhang Xun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yanjia_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yanjia-154">[154]</a></sup> Both were unsuccessful, but the "feudal remnants" returned to China with the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a> in a concept called <a href="/wiki/Guanxi" title="Guanxi">guanxi</a>, where people relied not on feudal relationships, but personal relationships, for survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yangm_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-yangm-155">[155]</a></sup> While guanxi is helpful in Taiwan, on the mainland, guanxi is necessary to get anything done.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luoy_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luoy-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_significance">Historical significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=69" title="Edit section: Historical significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Xinhai Revolution overthrew the Qing government and two thousand years of monarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xb1_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-xb1-2">[2]</a></sup> Throughout Chinese history, old dynasties had always been replaced by new dynasties. The Xinhai Revolution, however, was the first to overthrow a monarchy completely and attempt to establish a republic to spread democratic ideas throughout China. Though in 1911 at the provisional government welcome ceremony, Sun Yat-sen said, "The revolution is not yet successful, the <a href="/wiki/Comrade" title="Comrade">comrades</a> still need to strive for the future." (革命尚未成功，同志仍需努力).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since the 1920s, the two dominant parties–the KMT and PRC–see the Xinhai Revolution quite differently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scmpr_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-scmpr-158">[158]</a></sup> Both sides recognize Sun Yat-sen as the <a href="/wiki/Father_of_the_Nation" title="Father of the Nation">Father of the Nation</a>, but in Taiwan, they mean "Father of the Republic of China".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scmpr_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-scmpr-158">[158]</a></sup> On the mainland, Sun Yat-sen was seen as the man who helped bring down the Qing, a pre-condition for the Communist state founded in 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scmpr_158-2"><a href="#cite_note-scmpr-158">[158]</a></sup> The PRC views Sun's work as the first step towards the real revolution in 1949, when the communists set up a truly independent state that expelled foreigners and built a military and industrial power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scmpr_158-3"><a href="#cite_note-scmpr-158">[158]</a></sup> The father of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">New China</a> is seen as <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scmpr_158-4"><a href="#cite_note-scmpr-158">[158]</a></sup> In 1954, <a href="/wiki/Liu_Shaoqi" title="Liu Shaoqi">Liu Shaoqi</a> was quoted as saying that the "Xinhai Revolution inserted the concept of a republic into common people".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zhou_Enlai" title="Zhou Enlai">Zhou Enlai</a> pointed out that the "Xinhai Revolution overthrew the Qing rule, ended 2000 years of monarchy, and liberated the mind of people to a great extent, and opened up the path for the development of future revolution. This is a great victory."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_evaluation">Modern evaluation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=70" title="Edit section: Modern evaluation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taiw_100ann_10yuan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="534" data-file-width="1084" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Taiw_100ann_10yuan.jpg/300px-Taiw_100ann_10yuan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Taiw_100ann_10yuan.jpg/450px-Taiw_100ann_10yuan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Taiw_100ann_10yuan.jpg/600px-Taiw_100ann_10yuan.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Taiw_100ann_10yuan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Commemorative coin, minted in Taiwan in 2011</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A change in the belief that the revolution had been a generally positive change began in the late 1980s and 1990s, but <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Shizhao" title="Zhang Shizhao">Zhang Shizhao</a> was quoted as arguing that "When talking about the Xinhai Revolution, the theorist these days tends to overemphasize. The word 'success' was way overused."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>The success of the democracy gained from the revolution can vary depending on one's view. Even after the death of Sun Yat-sen in 1925, for sixty years, the KMT controlled all five branches of the government; none were independent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scmpjul4_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-scmpjul4-153">[153]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yan_Jiaqi" title="Yan Jiaqi">Yan Jiaqi</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Federation_for_a_Democratic_China" title="Federation for a Democratic China">Federation for a Democratic China</a>, has said that Sun Yat-sen is to be credited as founding China's first republic in 1912, and the second republic is the people of Taiwan and the political parties there now democratizing the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yanjia_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yanjia-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>Meanwhile, the ideals of democracy are far from realised on the mainland. For example, former Chinese premier <a href="/wiki/Wen_Jiabao" title="Wen Jiabao">Wen Jiabao</a> once said in a speech that without real democracy, there is no guarantee of economic and political rights; but he led a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2011_crackdown_on_dissidents_in_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="2011 crackdown on dissidents in the People's Republic of China">2011 crackdown</a> against the peaceful <a href="/wiki/2011_Chinese_pro-democracy_protests" title="2011 Chinese pro-democracy protests">Chinese jasmine protests</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Liu_Xiaobo" title="Liu Xiaobo">Liu Xiaobo</a>, a pro-democracy activist who received the global <a href="/wiki/2010_Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="2010 Nobel Peace Prize">2010 Nobel Peace Prize</a>, is in prison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> Others, such as Qin Yongmin (秦永敏) of the <a href="/wiki/Democracy_Party_of_China" title="Democracy Party of China">Democracy Party of China</a>, who was only released from prison after twelve years, do not praise the Xinhai Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geo_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-geo-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hrichina_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-hrichina-166">[166]</a></sup> Qin Yongmin said the revolution only replaced one dictator with another, that <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a> was not an emperor, but he is worse than the emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geo_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-geo-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hrichina_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-hrichina-166">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=71" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xinhai_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=72" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="refbegin" style="">
<p><span id="cnote_a"><b><a href="#ref_a">^</a></b></span> <b>a:</b> Many of the Qing soldiers with Han background turned to support the revolution during the uprisings, so the actual casualties are hard to trace.<br/>
<span id="cnote_b"><b><a href="#ref_b">^</a></b></span> <b>b:</b> Clipping from Min Bao (People's Papers). Originally the publishing of Hua Xin Hui and named <i>China of the Twentieth Century</i>, it was renamed after the establishment of Tongmenhui.<br/></p>
</div>

Taipei: Yuanliu (遠流). <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/957-32-4680-5" title="Special:BookSources/957-32-4680-5">957-32-4680-5</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AXinhai+Revolution&amp;rft.aufirst=Degang+%28%E5%BE%B7%E5%89%9B%29&amp;rft.aulast=Tang+%28%E5%94%90%29&amp;rft.btitle=%E8%A2%81%E6%B0%8F%E7%95%B6%E5%9C&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=957-32-4680-5&amp;rft.pub=Taipei%3A+Yuanliu+%28%E9%81%A0%E6%B5%81%29&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
<li><cite class="citation book">Zhang (張), Yufa (玉法) (1998). <bdi lang="zh">中華民國史稿</bdi> [<i>The History of the Republic of China</i>]. Taipei: Lianjin (聯經). <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/957-08-1826-3" title="Special:BookSources/957-08-1826-3">957-08-1826-3</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AXinhai+Revolution&amp;rft.aufirst=Yufa+%28%E7%8E%89%E6%B3%95%29&amp;rft.aulast=Zhang+%28%E5%BC%B5%29&amp;rft.btitle=%E4%B8%E8%8F%AF%E6%B0%91%E5%9C%E5%8F%B2%E7%A8%BF&amp;rft.date=1998&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=957-08-1826-3&amp;rft.pub=Taipei%3A+Lianjin+%28%E8%81%AF%E7%B6%93%29&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
<li><cite class="citation book">Lin (林), Yusheng (毓生) (1983). <bdi lang="zh">&lt;五四時代的激烈反傳統思想與中國自由主義的前途&gt; 收入"思想與人物"</bdi> [<i>The Anti-tradition Trends of May Forth Era and the Future of Libertarianism in China included in "Personage and their thoughts"</i>]. Taipei: Lianjin (聯經). <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/957-08-0384-3" title="Special:BookSources/957-08-0384-3">957-08-0384-3</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AXinhai+Revolution&amp;rft.aufirst=Yusheng+%28%E6%AF%93%E7%94%9F%29&amp;rft.aulast=Lin+%28%E6%9E%97%29&amp;rft.btitle=%3C%E4%BA%94%E5%9B%9B%E6%99%82%E4%BB%A3%E7%9A%84%E6%BF%E7%83%88%E5%8F%E5%82%B3%E7%B5%B1%E6%9D%E6%83%B3%E8%88%87%E4%B8%E5%9C%E8%87%AA%E7%94%B1%E4%B8%BB%E7%BE%A9%E7%9A%84%E5%89%E9%94%3E+%E6%94%B6%E5%85%A5%22%E6%9D%E6%83%B3%E8%88%87%E4%BA%BA%E7%89%A9%22&amp;rft.date=1983&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=957-08-0384-3&amp;rft.pub=Taipei%3A+Lianjin+%28%E8%81%AF%E7%B6%93%29&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
<li><cite class="citation book">Zhou (周), Weimin (伟民); Tang (唐), Linlin (玲玲) (2002). <bdi lang="zh">中国和马来西亚文化交流史</bdi> [<i>The History of Cultural Interactions of China and Malaysia</i>]. <a href="/wiki/Haikou" title="Haikou">Haikou</a>: Hainan (海南). <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/7-5443-0682-8" title="Special:BookSources/7-5443-0682-8">7-5443-0682-8</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AXinhai+Revolution&amp;rft.aufirst=Weimin+%28%E4%BC%9F%E6%B0%91%29&amp;rft.aulast=Zhou+%28%E5%91%A8%29&amp;rft.au=Tang+%28%E5%94%90%29%2C+Linlin+%28%E7%8E%B2%E7%8E%B2%29&amp;rft.btitle=%E4%B8%E5%9B%BD%E5%92%8C%E9%A9%AC%E6%9D%A5%E8%A5%BF%E4%BA%9A%E6%96%87%E5%8C%96%E4%BA%A4%E6%B5%81%E5%8F%B2&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=7-5443-0682-8&amp;rft.pub=Haikou%3A+Hainan+%28%E6%B5%B7%E5%97%29&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
